<p>The <b>moa</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> were nine <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> (in six genera) of <a href="/wiki/Flightless_bird" title="Flightless bird">flightless birds</a> endemic to New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bird.org.nz_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-bird.org.nz-5">[5]</a></sup> The two largest species, <i><a href="/wiki/Dinornis" title="Dinornis">Dinornis</a> robustus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dinornis" title="Dinornis">Dinornis</a> novaezelandiae</i>, reached about 3.6 m (12 ft) in height with neck outstretched, and weighed about 230 kg (510 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-6">[6]</a></sup> When Polynesians settled New Zealand around CE 1280, the moa population was about 58,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Moa belong to the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <b>Dinornithiformes</b>, traditionally placed in the <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">ratite</a> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bird.org.nz_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-bird.org.nz-5">[5]</a></sup> However, their closest relatives have been found by genetic studies to be the flighted South American <a href="/wiki/Tinamou" title="Tinamou">tinamous</a>, once considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister group</a> to ratites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips-8">[8]</a></sup> The nine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bird.org.nz_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-bird.org.nz-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[note 1]</a></sup> species of moa were the only wingless birds lacking even the vestigial wings which all other ratites have. They were the dominant <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a> in New Zealand's forest, shrubland and subalpine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a> for thousands of years, and until the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māori</a> were hunted only by the <a href="/wiki/Haast%27s_eagle" title="Haast's eagle">Haast's eagle</a>. Moa extinction occurred around CE 1300<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> - CE 1440 ± 20 years, primarily due to overhunting by Māori.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1588" data-file-width="2285" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png/220px-Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png/330px-Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png/440px-Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dinornithidae_SIZE_01.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A size comparison between 4 moa species and a human.
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<b>1.</b> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinornis_novaezealandiae" title="Dinornis novaezealandiae">Dinornis novaezealandiae</a></i>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<b>2.</b> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emeus_crassus" title="Emeus crassus">Emeus crassus</a></i>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<b>3.</b> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anomalopteryx_didiformis" title="Anomalopteryx didiformis">Anomalopteryx didiformis</a></i>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<b>4.</b> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinornis_robustus" title="Dinornis robustus">Dinornis robustus</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although moa skeletons were traditionally reconstructed in an upright position to create impressive height, analysis of their vertebral articulation indicates that they probably carried their heads forward,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup> in the manner of a <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">kiwi</a>. The spine was attached to the rear of the head rather than the base, indicating the horizontal alignment. This would have allowed them to graze on low-elevation vegetation, while being able to lift their heads and browse trees when necessary. This has resulted in a reconsideration of the height of larger moa.</p>
<p>Although there is no surviving record of what sounds moa made, some idea of their calls can be gained from fossil evidence. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrate_trachea" title="Vertebrate trachea">trachea</a> of moa were supported by many small rings of bone known as tracheal rings. Excavation of these rings from articulated skeletons has shown that at least two moa genera (<i>Euryapteryx</i> and <i>Emeus</i>) exhibited tracheal elongation, that is, their trachea were up to 1 metre (3 ft) long and formed a large loop within the body cavity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup> These are the only ratites known to exhibit this feature, which is also present in several other bird groups including <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">swans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crane_(bird)" title="Crane (bird)">cranes</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_fowl" title="Guinea fowl">guinea fowl</a>. The feature is associated with deep, resonant vocalisations that can travel long distances.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_relationships">Evolutionary relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Evolutionary relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kiwi,_ostrich,_Dinornis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1198" data-file-width="992" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kiwi%2C_ostrich%2C_Dinornis.jpg/170px-Kiwi%2C_ostrich%2C_Dinornis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kiwi%2C_ostrich%2C_Dinornis.jpg/255px-Kiwi%2C_ostrich%2C_Dinornis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kiwi%2C_ostrich%2C_Dinornis.jpg/340px-Kiwi%2C_ostrich%2C_Dinornis.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kiwi,_ostrich,_Dinornis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A comparison of a <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">kiwi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostrich</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Dinornis" title="Dinornis">Dinornis</a></i>, each with its egg.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Research published starting in 2010 has found that the moa's closest cousins are small terrestrial South American birds called the <a href="/wiki/Tinamou" title="Tinamou">tinamous</a> which are able to fly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allentoft2012_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allentoft2012-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell2014_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2014-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker2014_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker2014-14">[13]</a></sup> Previously, the <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">kiwi</a>, the Australian <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cassowary" title="Cassowary">cassowary</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turvey_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turvey-15">[14]</a></sup> were thought to be most closely related to the moa.</p>
<p>Although dozens of species were described in the late 19th century and early 20th century, many were based on partial skeletons and turned out to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synonym_(zoology)" title="Synonym (zoology)">synonyms</a>. Currently, eleven species are formally recognised, although recent studies using <a href="/wiki/Ancient_DNA" title="Ancient DNA">ancient DNA</a> recovered from bones in museum collections suggest that distinct lineages exist within some of these. One factor that has caused much confusion in moa taxonomy is the intraspecific variation of bone sizes, between glacial and inter-glacial periods (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bergmann%E2%80%99s_rule" title="Bergmann’s rule">Bergmann’s rule</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allen%E2%80%99s_rule" title="Allen’s rule">Allen’s rule</a>) as well as <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a> being evident in several species. <i><a href="/wiki/Dinornis" title="Dinornis">Dinornis</a></i> seems to have had the most pronounced <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, with females being up to 150% as tall and 280% as heavy as males—so much bigger that they were formerly classified as separate species until 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huynen_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huynen-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bunce_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bunce-17">[16]</a></sup> A 2009 study showed that <i>Euryapteryx curtus</i> and <i>Euryapteryx gravis</i> were synonyms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bunce2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bunce2-18">[17]</a></sup> A 2010 study explained size differences among them as sexual dimorphism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> A 2012 morphological study interpreted them as subspecies instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_DNA" title="Ancient DNA">Ancient DNA</a> analyses have determined that there were a number of cryptic evolutionary lineages in several moa genera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> These may eventually be classified as species or subspecies; <i>Megalapteryx benhami</i> (Archey) which is synonymised with <i>M. didinus</i> (Owen) because the bones of both share all essential characters. Size differences can be explained by a north-south <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">cline</a> combined with temporal variation such that specimens were larger during the Otiran glacial period (the last ice age in New Zealand). Similar temporal size variation is known for the North Island <i>Pachyornis mappini</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Some of the other size variation for moa species can probably be explained by similar geographic and temporal factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest moa remains come from the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Saint_Bathans_Fauna" title="Saint Bathans Fauna">Saint Bathans Fauna</a>. Known from multiple eggshells and hindlimb elements, these represent at least two species of already fairly large sized species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emeus_crassus_e_Pachyornis_elephantopus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1898" data-file-width="1424" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Emeus_crassus_e_Pachyornis_elephantopus.jpg/170px-Emeus_crassus_e_Pachyornis_elephantopus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Emeus_crassus_e_Pachyornis_elephantopus.jpg/255px-Emeus_crassus_e_Pachyornis_elephantopus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Emeus_crassus_e_Pachyornis_elephantopus.jpg/340px-Emeus_crassus_e_Pachyornis_elephantopus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emeus_crassus_e_Pachyornis_elephantopus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Emeus crassus</i> and <i>Pachyornis elephantopus</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anomalopteryx_didiformus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="427" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Anomalopteryx_didiformus.jpg/170px-Anomalopteryx_didiformus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Anomalopteryx_didiformus.jpg/255px-Anomalopteryx_didiformus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Anomalopteryx_didiformus.jpg/340px-Anomalopteryx_didiformus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anomalopteryx_didiformus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Anomalopteryx didiformis</i> skeleton</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The currently recognised genera and species are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<ul>
<li>Order †<b>Dinornithiformes</b> <small>(Gadow 1893) Ridgway 1901</small> [Dinornithes <small>Gadow 1893</small>; Immanes <small>Newton 1884</small>] (moa)
<ul>
<li>Family <b>Dinornithidae</b> Owen 1843 [Palapteryginae Bonaparte 1854; Palapterygidae Haast 1874; Dinornithnideae Stejneger 1884]
<ul>
<li>Genus <i><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_moa" title="Giant moa">Dinornis</a></b></i>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/North_Island_giant_moa" title="North Island giant moa">North Island giant moa</a>, <i>Dinornis novaezealandiae</i> (North Island, New Zealand)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/South_Island_giant_moa" title="South Island giant moa">South Island giant moa</a>, <i>Dinornis robustus</i> (South Island, New Zealand)</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Family <b>Emeidae</b> <small>(Bonaparte 1854)</small> [Emeinae <small>Bonaparte 1854</small>; Anomalopterygidae <small>Oliver 1930</small>; Anomalapteryginae <small>Archey 1941</small>] (lesser moas)
<ul>
<li>Genus <i><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anomalopteryx" title="Anomalopteryx">Anomalopteryx</a></b></i>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bush_moa" title="Bush moa">Bush moa</a>, <i>Anomalopteryx didiformis</i> (South Island, New Zealand)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Genus <i><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emeus" title="Emeus">Emeus</a></b></i>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_moa" title="Eastern moa">Eastern moa</a>, <i>Emeus crassus</i> (South Island, New Zealand)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Genus <i><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euryapteryx" title="Euryapteryx">Euryapteryx</a></b></i>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coastal_moa" title="Coastal moa">Coastal moa</a>, <i>Euryapteryx curtus</i> (North and South Island, New Zealand)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Genus <i><b><a href="/wiki/Pachyornis" title="Pachyornis">Pachyornis</a></b></i>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Heavy-footed_moa" title="Heavy-footed moa">Heavy-footed moa</a>, <i>Pachyornis elephantopus</i> (South Island, New Zealand)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mantell%27s_moa" title="Mantell's moa">Mantell's moa</a>, <i>Pachyornis geranoides</i> (North Island, New Zealand)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crested_moa" title="Crested moa">Crested moa</a>, <i>Pachyornis australis</i> (South Island, New Zealand)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephenson_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stephenson-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Family <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megalapterygidae" title="Megalapterygidae">Megalapterygidae</a></b>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">Genus</a> <i><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megalapteryx" title="Megalapteryx">Megalapteryx</a></b></i>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Upland_moa" title="Upland moa">Upland moa</a>, <i>Megalapteryx didinus</i> (South Island, New Zealand)</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Two unnamed species from the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Bathans_Fauna" title="Saint Bathans Fauna">Saint Bathans Fauna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Phylogeny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moa_footprints.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1811" data-file-width="1314" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Moa_footprints.jpg/170px-Moa_footprints.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Moa_footprints.jpg/255px-Moa_footprints.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Moa_footprints.jpg/340px-Moa_footprints.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moa_footprints.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Preserved footprints of a <i>D. robustus</i> found in 1911.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because <b>moa</b> are a group of flightless <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a> with no vestiges of wing bones, questions have been raised about how they arrived in New Zealand, and from where. There are many theories about the moa's arrival and radiation on New Zealand, but the most recent theory suggests that the moa arrived on New Zealand about 60 million years ago (Mya) and split from the "basal" (see below) moa species, <i>Megalapteryx</i> about 5.8 Mya<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> instead of the 18.5 Mya split suggested by Baker et al. (2005). This does not necessarily mean there was no speciation between the arrival 60 Mya and the basal split 5.8 Mya, but the fossil record is lacking and is it most likely that early moa lineages existed but became extinct before the basal split 5.8 Mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> The presence of <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> aged species certainly suggests that moa diversification began before the split between <i>Megalapteryx" and the other taxa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-24">[23]</a></sup></i></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> Drowning Maximum event, which occurred about 22 Mya, when only 18% of present-day New Zealand was above sea level, is very important in the moa radiation. Because the basal moa split occurred so recently (5.8 Mya), it was argued that ancestors of the Quaternary moa lineages could not have been present on both the South and North island remnants during the Oligocene drowning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> This does not imply that moa were previously absent from the North Island, but that only those from the South Island survived, because only the South Island was above sea level. Bunce et al. (2009) argued that moa ancestors survived on the South Island and then recolonized the North Island about 2 My later, when the two islands rejoined after 30 My of separation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bunce_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bunce-17">[16]</a></sup> The presence of Miocene moas in the Saint Bathans fauna seems to suggest that these birds increased in size soon after the Oligocene Drowning Event, if they were affected by it at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bunce et al. also concluded that the highly complex structure of the moa lineage was caused by the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Alps" title="Southern Alps">Southern Alps</a> about 6 Mya, and the habitat fragmentation on both islands resulting from Pleistocene glacial cycles, <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a>, and landscape changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bunce_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bunce-17">[16]</a></sup> On the left is a phylogeny of Palaeognathae generated by Mitchell (2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell2014_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell2014-13">[12]</a></sup> with some clade names after Yuri <i>et al.</i> (2013)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> The below right is a species level phylogeny of Dinornithiformes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bunce_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bunce-17">[16]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Analyses of fossil moa bone assemblages have provided detailed data on the habitat preferences of individual moa species, and revealed distinctive regional moa faunas:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Island">South Island</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: South Island">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giant_moa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1532" data-file-width="1616" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Giant_moa.jpg/170px-Giant_moa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Giant_moa.jpg/255px-Giant_moa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Giant_moa.jpg/340px-Giant_moa.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giant_moa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A restoration of <i>Dinornis robustus</i> and <i>Pachyornis elephantopus</i>, both from the South Island.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The two main faunas identified in the South Island include: 1. The fauna of the high rainfall west coast beech (<i><a href="/wiki/Nothofagus" title="Nothofagus">Nothofagus</a></i>) forests that included <i>Anomalopteryx didiformis</i> (bush moa) and <i>Dinornis robustus</i> (South Island giant moa); and 2. The fauna of the dry rainshadow forest and shrublands east of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Alps" title="Southern Alps">Southern Alps</a> that included <i>Pachyornis elephantopus</i> (heavy-footed moa), <i>Euryapteryx gravis</i>, <i>Emeus crassus</i> and <i>Dinornis robustus</i>. The two other moa species that existed in the South Island; <i>Pachyornis australis</i> and <i>Megalapteryx didinus</i> might be included in a ‘<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subalpine" title="Subalpine">subalpine</a> fauna’, along with the widespread <i>Dinornis robustus</i>. <i>P. australis</i> is the rarest of the moa species, and the only one not yet found in Maori middens.</p>
<p>Its bones have been found in caves in the northwest <a href="/wiki/Nelson,_New_Zealand" title="Nelson, New Zealand">Nelson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karamea" title="Karamea">Karamea</a> districts (such as <a href="/wiki/Honeycomb_Hill_Cave" title="Honeycomb Hill Cave">Honeycomb Hill Cave</a>), and some sites around the <a href="/wiki/Wanaka" title="Wanaka">Wanaka</a> district. <i>M. didinus</i> is more widespread. Its name "upland moa" reflects the fact its bones are commonly found in the subalpine zone. However, it also occurred down to sea level where there was suitable steep and rocky terrain (such as <a href="/wiki/Punakaiki" title="Punakaiki">Punakaiki</a> on the west coast and <a href="/wiki/Central_Otago" title="Central Otago">Central Otago</a>). Their distributions in coastal areas have been rather unclear, but were present at least in several locations such as on <a href="/wiki/Kaikoura" title="Kaikoura">Kaikoura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otago_Peninsula" title="Otago Peninsula">Otago Peninsula</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Karitane" title="Karitane">Karitane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_skeletons_of_Eastern_moa_and_other_kinds_of_moas_in_Otago_museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2804" data-file-width="1865" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/The_skeletons_of_Eastern_moa_and_other_kinds_of_moas_in_Otago_museum.jpg/170px-The_skeletons_of_Eastern_moa_and_other_kinds_of_moas_in_Otago_museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/The_skeletons_of_Eastern_moa_and_other_kinds_of_moas_in_Otago_museum.jpg/255px-The_skeletons_of_Eastern_moa_and_other_kinds_of_moas_in_Otago_museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/The_skeletons_of_Eastern_moa_and_other_kinds_of_moas_in_Otago_museum.jpg/340px-The_skeletons_of_Eastern_moa_and_other_kinds_of_moas_in_Otago_museum.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_skeletons_of_Eastern_moa_and_other_kinds_of_moas_in_Otago_museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The skeletons of Eastern moa (Emeus crassus) and other kinds of moas in Otago museum.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Island">North Island</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: North Island">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Significantly less is known about North Island paleofaunas, due to a paucity of fossil sites compared to the South Island; however, the basic pattern of moa-habitat relationships was the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup> Although the South Island and the North Island shared some moa species (<i>Euryapteryx gravis</i>, <i>Anomalopteryx didiformis</i>), most were exclusive to one island, reflecting divergence over several thousand years since lower sea level had resulted in a land bridge across <a href="/wiki/Cook_Strait" title="Cook Strait">Cook Strait</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the North Island, <i>Dinornis novaezealandiae</i> and <i>Anomalopteryx didiformis</i> dominated in high rainfall forest habitat; a similar pattern to the South Island. The other moa species present in the North Island (<i>Euryapteryx gravis</i>, <i>E. curtus</i>, and <i>Pachyornis geranoides</i>) tended to inhabit drier forest and shrubland habitats. <i>P. geranoides</i> occurred throughout the North Island, while the distributions of <i>E. gravis</i> and <i>E. curtus</i> were almost mutually exclusive, the former having only been found in coastal sites around the southern half of the North Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour_and_ecology">Behaviour and ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Behaviour and ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Approximately eight moa <a href="/wiki/Fossil_trackway" title="Fossil trackway">trackways</a>, with fossilised moa footprint impressions in fluvial silts have been found throughout the North Island, including Waikanae Creek (1872), Napier (1887), Manawatu River (1895), Marton (1896), Palmerston North (1911) (see photograph to left), Rangitikei River (1939), and underwater in Lake Taupo (1973). Analysis of the spacing of these tracks indicates walking speeds of between 3 and 5 km/h (1.75–3 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dinornis_giganteus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="882" data-file-width="1785" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Dinornis_giganteus.jpg/220px-Dinornis_giganteus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Dinornis_giganteus.jpg/330px-Dinornis_giganteus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Dinornis_giganteus.jpg/440px-Dinornis_giganteus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dinornis_giganteus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>D. novaezealandiae</i> skull at the Museum für Naturkunde, Berlin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although feeding moa were never observed by scientists, their diet has been deduced from <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossilised</a> contents of their <a href="/wiki/Gizzard" title="Gizzard">gizzards</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-38">[37]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Coprolite" title="Coprolite">coprolites</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> as well as indirectly through morphological analysis of skull and beak, and <a href="/wiki/Isotope_analysis" title="Isotope analysis">stable isotope analysis</a> of their bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup> Moa fed on a range of plant species and plant parts, including fibrous twigs and leaves taken from low trees and shrubs. The beak of <i><a href="/wiki/Pachyornis" title="Pachyornis">Pachyornis</a></i> <i>elephantopus</i> was analogous to a pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secateur" title="Secateur">secateurs</a>, and was able to clip the fibrous leaves of New Zealand flax (<i><a href="/wiki/Phormium" title="Phormium">Phormium</a></i> <i>tenax</i>) and twigs up to at least 8 mm in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-38">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like many other birds, moa swallowed <i>gizzard stones</i> (<a href="/wiki/Gastrolith" title="Gastrolith">gastroliths</a>), which were retained in their muscular gizzards, providing a grinding action that allowed them to eat coarse plant material. These stones were commonly smooth, rounded quartz pebbles, but stones over 110 millimetres (4 in) in length have been found amongst preserved moa gizzard contents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-38">[37]</a></sup> <i>Dinornis</i> gizzards could often contain several kilograms of stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_skeleton_of_female_upland_moa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/The_skeleton_of_female_upland_moa.JPG/220px-The_skeleton_of_female_upland_moa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/The_skeleton_of_female_upland_moa.JPG/330px-The_skeleton_of_female_upland_moa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/The_skeleton_of_female_upland_moa.JPG/440px-The_skeleton_of_female_upland_moa.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_skeleton_of_female_upland_moa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The skeleton of female upland moa with egg in unlaid position within the pelvic cavity in Otago Museum.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It has been long suspected that the pairs of species of moa described as <i>Euryapteryx curtus</i>/<i>E. exilis</i>, <i>Emeus huttonii</i>/<i>E. crassus</i>, and <i>Pachyornis septentrionalis</i>/<i>P. mappini</i> constituted males and females, respectively. This has been confirmed by analysis for sex-specific genetic markers of DNA extracted from bone material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huynen_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Huynen-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>For example, prior to 2003 there were three species of <i>Dinornis</i> recognised: South Island giant moa (<i>D. robustus</i>), North Island giant moa (<i>D. novaezealandiae</i>) and slender moa (<i>D. struthioides</i>). However, DNA showed that all <i>D. struthioides</i> were in fact males, and all <i>D. robustus</i> were females. Therefore, the three species of <i>Dinornis</i> were reclassified as two species, one each formerly occurring on New Zealand's North Island (<i>D. novaezealandiae</i>) and South Island (<i>D. robustus</i>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huynen_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Huynen-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> <i>D. robustus</i> however, comprises three distinct genetic lineages and may eventually be classified as many species, as discussed above.</p>
<p>Examination of growth rings present in moa cortical bone has revealed that these birds were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K-selection" title="K-selection">K-selected</a>, as are many other large endemic New Zealand birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turvey_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Turvey-15">[14]</a></sup> They are characterised by having low <a href="/wiki/Fecundity" title="Fecundity">fecundity</a> and a long <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/maturation" title="wikt:maturation">maturation</a> period, taking approximately ten years to reach adult size. The large <i>Dinornis</i> species took the same length of time to reach adult size as small moa species, and as a result had an accelerated rate of skeletal growth during their juvenile years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turvey_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Turvey-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emeus_egg_and_embryo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3147" data-file-width="2274" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Emeus_egg_and_embryo.jpg/170px-Emeus_egg_and_embryo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Emeus_egg_and_embryo.jpg/255px-Emeus_egg_and_embryo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Emeus_egg_and_embryo.jpg/340px-Emeus_egg_and_embryo.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emeus_egg_and_embryo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An egg and embryo fragments of <i>Emeus crassus</i>,</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is no evidence to suggest that moa were colonial nesters. While evidence of moa nesting is often inferred from accumulations of eggshell fragments found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caves" title="Caves">caves</a> and rock shelters, little evidence exists of the <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">nests</a> themselves. Excavations of rock shelters in the eastern North Island during the 1940s uncovered moa nests, which were described as "small depressions obviously scratched out in the soft dry <a href="/wiki/Pumice" title="Pumice">pumice</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Moa nesting material has also been recovered from rock shelters in the <a href="/wiki/Central_Otago" title="Central Otago">Central Otago</a> region of the South Island, where the dry climate has resulted in the preservation of plant material used to construct the nesting platform (including twigs that have been clipped by moa bills).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-42">[41]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seeds" title="Seeds">Seeds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> within moa coprolites found amongst the nesting material provide evidence that the nesting season was late spring to summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fragments of moa eggshell are often encountered in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological" title="Archaeological">archaeological</a> sites and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sand_dunes" title="Sand dunes">sand dunes</a> around the New Zealand coast. Thirty six whole moa eggs exist in museum collections and vary greatly in size (from 120–240 millimetres (4.7–9.4 in) in length and 91–178 millimetres (3.6–7.0 in) wide).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> The outer surface of moa eggshell is characterised by small slit-shaped pores. The eggs of most moa species were white, although those of the upland moa (<i>Megalapteryx didinus</i>) were blue-green.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huynen.2C_Leon_2010_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huynen.2C_Leon_2010-44">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2010 study by Huynen et al. has found that the eggs of certain species were fragile, only around a millimeter in thickness: "Unexpectedly, several thin-shelled eggs were also shown to belong to the heaviest moa of the genera <i>Dinornis</i>, <i>Euryapteryx</i>, and <i>Emeus</i>, making these, to our knowledge, the most fragile of all avian eggs measured to date. Moreover, sex-specific DNA recovered from the outer surfaces of eggshells belonging to species of <i>Dinornis</i> and <i>Euryapteryx</i> suggest that these very thin eggs were likely to have been incubated by the lighter males. The thin nature of the eggshells of these larger species of moa, even if incubated by the male, suggests that egg breakage in these species would have been common if the typical contact method of avian egg incubation was used."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huynen.2C_Leon_2010_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Huynen.2C_Leon_2010-44">[43]</a></sup> Despite the bird's extinction, the high yield of DNA available from recovered fossilized eggs has allowed the moa to have its genome sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extinction">Extinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Extinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1101" data-file-width="1375" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg/220px-Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg/330px-Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg/440px-Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An artist's rendition of a <a href="/wiki/Haast%27s_eagle" title="Haast's eagle">Haast's eagle</a> attacking moa.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before the arrival of human settlers, the moa's only predator was the massive <a href="/wiki/Haast%27s_eagle" title="Haast's eagle">Haast's eagle</a>. New Zealand had been isolated for 80 million years and had few predators prior to human arrival, meaning that not only were its ecosystems extremely fragile but the native species were ill-equipped to cope with human predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māori</a> arrived sometime before CE 1300, and all moa genera were soon driven to extinction by hunting and, to a lesser extent, by habitat reduction due to forest clearance. By 1445, all moa had become extinct, along with the Haast's eagle which had relied on them for food. Recent research using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon-14_dating" title="Carbon-14 dating">carbon-14 dating</a> of <a href="/wiki/Midden" title="Midden">middens</a> strongly suggests that the events leading to extinction took less than a hundred years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> rather than a period of exploitation lasting several hundred years, which is what had previously been hypothesized.</p>
<p>Some authors have speculated that a few <i>Megalapteryx didinus</i> may have persisted in remote corners of New Zealand until the 18th and even 19th centuries, but this view is not widely accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-49">[48]</a></sup> Some Māori hunters claimed to be in pursuit of the moa as late as the 1770s. <a href="/wiki/Whaler" title="Whaler">Whalers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seal_hunter" title="Seal hunter">sealers</a> recalled seeing monstrous birds along the coast of the South Island, and in the 1820s, a man named George Pauley made an unverified claim of seeing a moa in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otago_Region" title="Otago Region">Otago Region</a> of New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>An expedition in the 1850s under Lieutenant A. Impey reported two emu-like birds on a hillside on the South Island; an 1861 story from the <i>Nelson Examiner</i> told of three-toed footprints measuring 36 centimetres (14 in) between <a href="/wiki/Takaka,_New_Zealand" title="Takaka, New Zealand">Takaka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Riwaka" title="Riwaka">Riwaka</a> that were found by a surveying party; and finally in 1878 the <i><a href="/wiki/Otago_Witness" title="Otago Witness">Otago Witness</a></i> published an additional account from a farmer and his shepherd.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-51">[50]</a></sup> An 80-year-old woman, Alice Mckenzie, claimed in 1959 that she had seen a moa in <a href="/wiki/Fiordland" title="Fiordland">Fiordland</a> bush in 1887, and again on a Fiordland beach when she was 17 years old. She claimed that her brother had also seen a moa on another occasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surviving_remains">Surviving remains</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Surviving remains">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dinornis1387.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2820" data-file-width="2268" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Dinornis1387.jpg/170px-Dinornis1387.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Dinornis1387.jpg/255px-Dinornis1387.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Dinornis1387.jpg/340px-Dinornis1387.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dinornis1387.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sir <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a> holding the first discovered moa fossil and standing with a <i>Dinornis</i> skeleton, 1879.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Joel_Samuel_Polack" title="Joel Samuel Polack">Joel Polack</a>, a trader who lived on the East Coast of the North Island from 1834 to 1837, recorded in 1838 that he had been shown "several large fossil ossifications" found near Mt Hikurangi. He was certain that these were the bones of a species of emu or ostrich, noting that "the Natives add that in times long past they received the traditions that very large birds had existed, but the scarcity of animal food, as well as the easy method of entrapping them, has caused their extermination". Polack further noted that he had received reports from Māori that a "species of Struthio" still existed in remote parts of the South Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dieffenbach<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> also refers to a fossil from the area near Mt Hikurangi, and surmises that it belongs to "a bird, now extinct, called Moa (or Movie) by the natives". In 1839 John W. Harris, a <a href="/wiki/Poverty_Bay" title="Poverty Bay">Poverty Bay</a> flax trader who was a natural history enthusiast, was given a piece of unusual bone by a Māori who had found it in a river bank. He showed the 15 centimetres (6 in) fragment of bone to his uncle, John Rule, a Sydney surgeon, who sent it to <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a>, who at that time was working at the Hunterian Museum at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Surgeons_of_England" title="Royal College of Surgeons of England">Royal College of Surgeons</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Owen puzzled over the fragment for almost four years. He established it was part of the <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a> of a big animal, but it was uncharacteristically light and honeycombed. Owen announced to a skeptical scientific community and the world that it was from a giant extinct bird like an <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostrich</a>, and named it <i>Dinornis</i>. His deduction was ridiculed in some quarters, but was proved correct with the subsequent discoveries of considerable quantities of moa bones throughout the country, sufficient to reconstruct skeletons of the birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_London" title="Natural History Museum, London">Natural History Museum</a> in London placed on display the moa bone fragment Owen had first examined, to celebrate 200 years since his birth, and in memory of Owen as founder of the museum.</p>
<p>Since the discovery of the first moa bones in the late 1830s, thousands more have been found. They occur in a range of late <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary" title="Sedimentary">sedimentary</a> deposits, but are most common in three main types of site: <a href="/wiki/Cave" title="Cave">caves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dune" title="Dune">dunes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swamp" title="Swamp">swamps</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graveyard_2_a.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="752" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Graveyard_2_a.JPG/220px-Graveyard_2_a.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Graveyard_2_a.JPG/330px-Graveyard_2_a.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Graveyard_2_a.JPG/440px-Graveyard_2_a.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graveyard_2_a.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Palaeontologists working on moa bone deposits in the 'Graveyard', Honeycomb Hill Cave System. This cave is a closed scientific reserve.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bones are commonly found in caves or ‘'tomo'’ (the Maori word for doline or <a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">sinkhole</a>; often used to refer to pitfalls or vertical cave shafts). The two main ways that the moa bones were deposited in such sites were: 1. birds that entered the cave to nest or escape bad weather, and subsequently died in the cave; and 2. birds that fell into a vertical shaft and were unable to escape. Moa bones (and the bones of other extinct birds) have been found in caves throughout New Zealand, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>/<a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> areas of northwest Nelson, <a href="/wiki/Karamea" title="Karamea">Karamea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waitomo" title="Waitomo">Waitomo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Te_Anau" title="Te Anau">Te Anau</a>.</p>
<p>Moa bones and eggshell fragments sometimes occur in active coastal sand dunes, where they may erode from <a href="/wiki/Paleosol" title="Paleosol">paleosols</a> and concentrate in ‘<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blowout_(geology)" title="Blowout (geology)">blowouts</a>’ between dune ridges. Many such moa bones predate human settlement, although some originate from Maori midden sites, which frequently occur in dunes near harbours and river mouths (for example the large moa hunter sites at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shag_River" title="Shag River">Shag River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otago" title="Otago">Otago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wairau_Bar" title="Wairau Bar">Wairau Bar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marlborough_Region" title="Marlborough Region">Marlborough</a>).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kapua_Swamp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="549" data-file-width="883" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Kapua_Swamp.jpg/220px-Kapua_Swamp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Kapua_Swamp.jpg/330px-Kapua_Swamp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Kapua_Swamp.jpg/440px-Kapua_Swamp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kapua_Swamp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An excavation in Kapua Swamp, 1894.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Densely intermingled moa bones have been encountered in swamps throughout New Zealand. The most well-known example is at <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_Valley" title="Pyramid Valley">Pyramid Valley</a> in north Canterbury,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> where bones from at least 183 individual moa have been excavated, mostly by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Duff" title="Roger Duff">Roger Duff</a> of <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Museum,_Christchurch" title="Canterbury Museum, Christchurch">Canterbury Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNZB_Duff_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNZB_Duff-57">[56]</a></sup> Many explanations have been proposed to account for how these deposits formed, ranging from poisonous spring waters to floods and wildfires. However the currently accepted explanation is that the bones accumulated at a slow rate over thousands of years, from birds that had entered the swamps to feed and became trapped in the soft sediment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feathers_and_soft_tissues">Feathers and soft tissues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Feathers and soft tissues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moaupland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="856" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Moaupland.jpg/170px-Moaupland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Moaupland.jpg/255px-Moaupland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Moaupland.jpg/340px-Moaupland.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moaupland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <i>Megalapteryx didinus</i> head.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several remarkable examples of moa remains have been found which exhibit soft tissues (<a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feathers" title="Feathers">feathers</a>), that were preserved through <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a> when the bird died in a naturally dry site (for example, a cave with a constant dry breeze blowing through it). Most of these specimens have been found in the semi-arid <a href="/wiki/Central_Otago" title="Central Otago">Central Otago</a> region, the driest part of New Zealand. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Dried muscle on bones of a female <i>Dinornis robustus</i> found at Tiger Hill in the <a href="/wiki/Manuherikia_River" title="Manuherikia River">Manuherikia River</a> Valley by gold miners in 1864<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owen-59">[58]</a></sup> (currently held by <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_Museum" title="Yorkshire Museum">Yorkshire Museum</a>)</li>
<li>Several bones of <i>Emeus crassus</i> with muscle attached, and a row of neck vertebrae with muscle, skin and feathers collected from Earnscleugh Cave near the town of Alexandra in 1870<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> (currently held by <a href="/wiki/Otago_Museum" title="Otago Museum">Otago Museum</a>)</li>
<li>An articulated foot of a male <i>Dinornis giganteus</i> with skin and foot pads preserved, found in a crevice on the Knobby Range in 1874<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buller_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buller-61">[60]</a></sup> (currently held by <a href="/wiki/Otago_Museum" title="Otago Museum">Otago Museum</a>)</li>
<li>The type specimen of <i>Megalapteryx didinus</i> found near <a href="/wiki/Queenstown,_New_Zealand" title="Queenstown, New Zealand">Queenstown</a> in 1878<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Owen-59">[58]</a></sup> (currently held by <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_Museum,_London" title="Natural History Museum, London">Natural History Museum</a>, London; see photograph of foot on this page)</li>
<li>The lower leg of <i>Pachyornis elephantopus</i>, with skin and muscle, from the Hector Range in 1884;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buller_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buller-61">[60]</a></sup> (currently held by the Zoology Department, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a>)</li>
<li>The complete feathered leg of a <i>Megalapteryx didinus</i> from Old Man Range in 1894<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> (currently held by <a href="/wiki/Otago_Museum" title="Otago Museum">Otago Museum</a>)</li>
<li>The head of a <i>Megalapteryx didinus</i> found near Cromwell sometime prior to 1949<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> (currently held by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museum_of_New_Zealand" title="Museum of New Zealand">Museum of New Zealand</a>).</li>
</ul>
<p>Two specimens are known from outside the Central Otago region:</p>
<ul>
<li>A complete foot of <i>Megalapteryx didinus</i> found in a cave on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Owen,_New_Zealand" title="Mount Owen, New Zealand">Mount Owen</a> near Nelson in 1980s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> (currently held by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museum_of_New_Zealand" title="Museum of New Zealand">Museum of New Zealand</a>)</li>
<li>A skeleton of <i>Anomalopteryx didiformis</i> with muscle, skin and feather bases collected from a cave near <a href="/wiki/Te_Anau" title="Te Anau">Te Anau</a> in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moa_foot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1572" data-file-width="2252" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Moa_foot.jpg/220px-Moa_foot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Moa_foot.jpg/330px-Moa_foot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Moa_foot.jpg/440px-Moa_foot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moa_foot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A preserved <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megalapteryx" title="Megalapteryx">Megalapteryx</a></i> foot, Natural History Museum.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to these specimens, loose moa feathers have been collected from caves and rockshelters in the southern South Island, and based on these remains, some idea of the moa plumage has been achieved. The preserved leg of <i>Megalapteryx didinus</i> from the Old Man Range reveals that this species was feathered right down to the foot. This is likely to have been an adaptation to living in high altitude, snowy environments, and is also seen in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darwin%E2%80%99s_rhea" title="Darwin’s rhea">Darwin’s rhea</a>, which lives in a similar seasonally snowy habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Moa feathers are up to 23 centimetres (9 in) long, and a range of colours have been reported, including reddish-brown, white, yellowish and purplish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_11-10"><a href="#cite_note-WH-11">[10]</a></sup> Dark feathers with white or creamy tips have also been found, and indicate that some moa species may have had plumage with a speckled appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_moa_survival">Claims of moa survival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Claims of moa survival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moa_mock_hunt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="635" data-file-width="521" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Moa_mock_hunt.jpg/170px-Moa_mock_hunt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Moa_mock_hunt.jpg/255px-Moa_mock_hunt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Moa_mock_hunt.jpg/340px-Moa_mock_hunt.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moa_mock_hunt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An early 20th century reconstruction of a moa hunt.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The moa is thought to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">extinct</a>, but there has been occasional speculation—since at least the late 19th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> and as recently as 1993<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnimalX_Moa_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnimalX_Moa-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> and 2008<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-72">[71]</a></sup>—that some moa may still exist, particularly in the rugged wilderness of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westland,_New_Zealand" title="Westland, New Zealand">South Westland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiordland" title="Fiordland">Fiordland</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cryptozoology" title="Cryptozoology">Cryptozoologists</a> and others reputedly continue to search for them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> but their claims and supporting evidence (such as of purported footprints<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-72">[71]</a></sup> or blurry photos) have earned little attention from mainstream experts, and are widely considered <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscientific</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-49">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rediscovery of the <a href="/wiki/Takah%C4%93" title="Takahē">takahē</a> in 1948 after none had been seen since 1898 showed that rare birds may exist undiscovered for a long time. However, the takahē is a much smaller bird than the moa, and was rediscovered after its tracks were identified—yet no reliable evidence of moa tracks has ever been found, and experts still contend that moa survival is extremely unlikely, since this would involve the ground-dwelling birds living unnoticed for over five hundred years in a region visited often by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter" title="Hunter">hunters</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hiker" title="Hiker">hikers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-72">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_revival">Potential revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Potential revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The creature has frequently been mentioned as <a href="/wiki/De-extinction" title="De-extinction">a potential candidate for revival by cloning</a>. Its iconic status, coupled with the facts that it only became extinct a few hundred years ago and that substantial quantities of moa remains exist, mean that it is often listed alongside such creatures as the <a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">dodo</a> as leading candidates for resurrection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> Preliminary work involving the extraction of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> has been undertaken by Japanese geneticist Ankoh Yasuyuki Shirota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Interest in the moa's potential for revival was further stirred in mid 2014 when New Zealand Member of Parliament <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Mallard" title="Trevor Mallard">Trevor Mallard</a> suggested that bringing back some smaller species of moa within 50 years was a viable idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> The idea was ridiculed by many, but gained support from some natural history experts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Moa_in_literature_and_culture">Moa in literature and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Moa in literature and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Harder" title="Heinrich Harder">Heinrich Harder</a> portrayed moa being hunted by Māori in the classic German collecting cards about extinct and prehistoric animals, "Tiere der Urwelt", in the early 1900s.</p>
<p>A poem about moa, "The Skeleton of the Great Moa in the Canterbury Museum, Christchurch" by Allen Curnow,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> was released by The 1979 Anthology of New Zealand Poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reconstructed Moa meat (particularly drum sticks) figures as a minor plot device in several of <a href="/wiki/Larry_Niven" title="Larry Niven">Larry Niven's</a> <a href="/wiki/Known_Space" title="Known Space">Known Space</a> stories.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_New_Zealand_animals#Birds" title="List of extinct New Zealand animals">List of extinct New Zealand animals</a> (birds)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Moa-nalo" title="Moa-nalo">Moa-nalo</a>, several flightless ducks from the Hawaiian Islands that grew to be as large as geese.</li>
</ul>

<ul>

</ul>
